<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="abd331e90520495f99bc26fdaaaec386">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'fd700f18-5c43-45a2-9749-0b24091277ea'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d68825728" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A new sodium single-ion conducting polymer electrolyte (SIPE) enables high rate capability and stable cycling of Na‖Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cells with an average Coulombic efficiency of 99.9 % and a capacity retention of more than 84 % after 1,000 cycles.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d9066139-d419-4f3e-bc18-37ed97084db4/anie202308699-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d9066139-d419-4f3e-bc18-37ed97084db4/anie202308699-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/98a96a5d-3b59-40dc-a5af-5e60904d6f72/anie202308699-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d9066139-d419-4f3e-bc18-37ed97084db4/anie202308699-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d68825775" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-metal batteries (SMBs) are considered a potential alternative to high-energy lithium-metal batteries (LMBs). However, the high reactivity of metallic sodium towards common liquid organic electrolytes renders such battery technology particularly challenging. Herein, we propose a multi-block single-ion conducting polymer electrolyte (SIPE) doped with ethylene carbonate as suitable electrolyte system for SMBs. This novel SIPE provides a very high ionic conductivity (2.6 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>) and an electrochemical stability window of about 4.1 V at 40 °C, enabling stable sodium stripping and plating and excellent rate capability of Na||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cells up to 2 C. Remarkably, such cells provide a capacity retention of about 85 % after 1,000 cycles at 0.2 C thanks to the very high Coulombic efficiency (99.9 %), resulting from an excellent interfacial stability towards sodium metal and the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202308699-sec-0001">
         
         <p>Low-cost and high-efficiency energy storage devices are considered key for the successful transition to renewable energy sources.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0001_R_d68825577e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Over the past decades, lithium-based batteries have dominated the electrochemical energy storage market and are nowadays widely applied in portable electronics, (hybrid) electric vehicles, and also stationary storage systems.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0005_R_d68825577e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> However, this exceptional success also raised concerns about the availability of the elements and compounds required with regard to the essentially exponential rise in lithium battery sales, including lithium.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0009_R_d68825577e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Therefore, batteries based on sodium cations as the charge carrier have attracted an increasing awareness, which very recently resulted in the announcement of several companies around the globe to commercialize sodium-ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0016_R_d68825577e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Among the key advantages of sodium batteries compared to lithium batteries are the almost unlimited abundance of sodium and its very homogeneous distribution around the world as well as the use of aluminum as current collector for both the electrodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0011_R_d68825577e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3b - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3b</a>, <a href="#anie202308699-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0015_R_d68825577e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3f - Chem">3f</a>, <a href="#anie202308699-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0020_R_d68825577e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> Their energy density, however, is significantly lower than that of lithium-ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0026_R_d68825577e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> One possibility to address this issue might be the use of metallic sodium at the negative electrode, offering a high theoretical specific capacity of 1,165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> along with a low redox potential of −2.71 V vs. the standard hydrogen electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0029_R_d68825577e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, sodium-metal electrodes (similar to lithium-metal electrodes) suffer from the risk of dendritic sodium deposition and high reactivity with the conventional organic liquid electrolytes, which leads to continuous capacity loss and a relatively low Coulombic efficiency (CE).<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0034_R_d68825577e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> The risk of dendrite formation is particularly pronounced at elevated currents approaching or even exceeding the limiting current density owing to the depletion of sodium cations at the negative electrode surface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0038_R_d68825577e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> Once the dendrites have penetrated the separator and reached the opposite positive electrode, the resulting short circuit leads to severe safety issues.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0042_R_d68825577e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> One approach to overcome this issue relies on the design of single-ion conducting electrolytes with a sodium transference number (t<sub>Na+</sub>) of (close to) unity, for which the Sand's time is theoretically approaching infinity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0041_R_d68825577e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9c - Energy Environ. Sci.">9c</a>, <a href="#anie202308699-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0049_R_d68825577e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">11</a></sup></span> This can be realized in polymers, for instance, by covalently tethering the anionic group to the polymer backbone, leaving only the cation for the effective charge transport in the electrolyte.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0050_R_d68825577e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> Achieving sufficiently high ionic conductivity (&gt;0.1 mS cm<sup>−2</sup>), though, is a great challenge in this case.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0054_R_d68825577e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Following our work on single-ion conducting polymer electrolytes (SIPEs) for lithium batteries,<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0058_R_d68825577e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> we report herein a sodium SIPE doped with ethylene carbonate (EC) that shows a very high ionic conductivity and suitable electrochemical stability window. This SIPE enables the realization of Na||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cells providing very good rate capability and excellent cycling stability for 1,000 cycles.</p>
         
         <p>The molecular structure of the multi-block copolymer sodium SIPE is given in Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0001">1a</a>. The synthesis is described in detail in the Supporting Information and involves essentially three steps, i.e., the synthesis of the polymer backbone (Figure S1), its bromination (Figure S2), and the attachment of the ionic side chains (Figure S3). The eventual structure comprises weakly coordinating anionic side chains in the ionophilic block, enabling a facile dissociation of the sodium cations, and rigid, nonpolar ionophobic blocks that are non-miscible with the ionophilic blocks, ensuring suitable mechanical properties and, thus, allowing for the realization of free-standing polymer membranes. The charge transport is further supported by the incorporation of small molecules with a high dielectric constant that can coordinate the sodium cations and by this lower the activation energy for the jump from one anionic site to another. Via solvent casting, transparent, uniform, and self-standing membranes with a thickness of around 80 μm were obtained (Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0001">1b</a>). These membranes were doped with 65 wt.% EC, which resulted in a significant increase in thickness by about 200 % (Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0001">1b</a>). The EC content was confirmed by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA), as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0002">2a</a> (see also Figure S4 for pure EC as reference), revealing the EC evaporation prior to the thermal decomposition of the ionomer at about 400 °C. Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0002">2b</a> displays the differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) data. The DSC trace shows one sharp endothermic peak at around 33 °C resulting from the melting of EC and a glass transition temperature (T<sub>g</sub>) well below −20 °C, demonstrating good comparability with the T<sub>g</sub> of earlier reported ionomers applied in LMBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0058_R_d68825577e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> The ionic conductivity as a function of temperature is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0002">2c</a>. Above 20 °C where the ionic conductivity is hampered by (partially) crystallized EC,<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0059_R_d68825577e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14a - Energy Environ. Sci.">14a</a></sup></span> it jumps to &gt;1 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>. At 40 and 80 °C, for instance, the conductivity is as high as 2.6 and 5.5 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which is comparable to conventional liquid electrolytes - not least with respect to the substantially lower Na<sup>+</sup> transference number of such liquid systems.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0061_R_d68825577e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Electrochim. Acta">15</a></sup></span> Similarly, a very high limiting current density of 5.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> was recorded at 40 °C (Figure S5), which is well above the lithium analogues of such SIPEs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0058_R_d68825577e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a>, <a href="#anie202308699-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0062_R_d68825577e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Energy">16</a></sup></span> The electrochemical stability of the EC-doped SIPE was evaluated via linear sweep voltammetry at 40 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0002">2d</a>). Towards reduction, only a very low current below 0.8 V was observed, presumably resulting from the decomposition of EC at the interface with the sodium-metal electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0063_R_d68825577e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span> Towards oxidation, the current remains essentially zero before sharply increasing beyond 4 V. If we consider an evolving current of 25 μA cm<sup>−2</sup> as the threshold, an anodic stability of 4.1 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na was found, which appears sufficiently wide for the combination with, e.g., NVP as a well-known sodium battery cathode material with a desodiation potential of about 3.4 V.<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0064_R_d68825577e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">18</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202308699-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4d6d0441-a79a-4349-95ea-d4c87f34a3f7/anie202308699-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4d6d0441-a79a-4349-95ea-d4c87f34a3f7/anie202308699-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d8ec9715-4083-468b-a0c7-52b6def6cb55/anie202308699-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4d6d0441-a79a-4349-95ea-d4c87f34a3f7/anie202308699-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202308699-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202308699" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Chemical structure of the sodium ionomer. (b) Photographs of the EC-free and EC-doped ionomer membranes with an indication of their thickness.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202308699-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/56e8dd02-7fe0-46e4-bd08-d71319598663/anie202308699-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/56e8dd02-7fe0-46e4-bd08-d71319598663/anie202308699-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2199c8eb-eaa5-45e0-b817-a84ac7b4d215/anie202308699-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/56e8dd02-7fe0-46e4-bd08-d71319598663/anie202308699-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202308699-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202308699" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) TGA data recorded for the dry ionomer (dashed line) and the EC-doped membranes (solid line) with an indication of the EC content. (b) DSC thermogram recorded for the EC-doped ionomer membrane with an indication of the melting point of EC and the T<sub>g</sub> (see also a magnification of the indicated region as inset; sweep rate: 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>). (c) Temperature-dependent ionic conductivity of the EC-doped ionomer. (d) LSV plot for the determination of the electrochemical stability with the cathodic sweep in black and the anodic sweep in red (sweep rate: 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; temperature: 40 °C).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In a first step, though, we evaluated the stability towards sodium-metal electrodes upon stripping and plating in symmetric Na||Na cells (Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0003">3</a>). The test with varying current densities ranging from 5 to 50 μA cm<sup>−2</sup> is depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0003">3a</a> along with a magnification of selected stripping/plating cycles for all current densities in Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0003">3b</a>. Generally, the overpotential remains rather stable independent of the applied current density, after an initial slight decrease in overpotential. This initial decrease appears to be related to an initial decrease in interfacial resistance, as revealed by an additional constant current stripping/plating experiment at 50 μA cm<sup>−2</sup> accompanied by continuous electrochemical impedance spectroscopy measurements each 10 stripping and plating cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0003">3c</a>). The interfacial impedance (<i>R</i><sub>i</sub>) first increases slightly from the 1<sup>st</sup> to the 3<sup>rd</sup> EIS measurement, before it continuously decreases upon further stripping and plating and eventually stabilizes after about 190 cycles at about 7,300 Ω along with a decrease and stabilization in overpotential (Figure S6). This stabilization indicates the formation of a stable interface (and presumably interphase) between the SIPE and the metallic sodium electrodes. The relatively high <i>R</i><sub>i</sub> can be attributed to the rather resistive interphase on the pristine sodium metal resulting from the processing of bulk sodium metal into foils as well as an interfacial contribution resulting from a spontaneous reaction between the EC-doped SIPE and the sodium-metal electrodes. This resistive interphase hinders the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport across the interface, as also reflected by the generally rather high overpotential of up to about 0.2 V at 50 μA cm<sup>−2</sup>, as also reported earlier for other sodium polymer electrolytes (Table S1). Nonetheless, the voltage response of the stripping/plating cycles remains very flat at each current density, as expected for a single-ion conducting electrolyte system. The single-ion conductivity was further confirmed by the determination of the cationic transference number according to the method reported by Bruce, Vincent and Evans,<span><sup><a href="#anie202308699-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202308699-bib-0065_R_d68825577e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Polymer">19</a></sup></span> revealing a value of 0.96 (Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0003">3d</a>), which is very close to unity. In fact, the current remains highly stable for 5,000 s and the impedance before and after polarization are very well overlapping, further indicating the formation of a stable electrode|electrolyte interface. Finally, we evaluated the performance of this SIPE in SMB configuration with NVP cathodes, i.e., in Na|SIPE|NVP cells (Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0004">4</a>). With regard to the ionic conductivity, the testing was conducted at 40 °C in order to ensure a high ionic conductivity of the SIPE in such cells. The active material mass loading was about 2.8±0.1 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, and a C rate of 1 C corresponds to a specific current of 120 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and a current density of 0.336 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. Remarkably, the cells showed high specific capacities of 107 and 104 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and 1 C, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0004">4a</a>). Even at 2 C, the cells still provided a very high reversible capacity of 100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which corresponds to about 94 % of the initial capacity at 0.05 C, indicating an excellent rate capability of such cells. When the dis-/charge rate was decreased back to 0.3 C, a specific capacity of 106 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained, i.e., exactly the same capacity as prior to the application of the elevated dis-/charge rates. The corresponding dis-/charge profiles for the different C rates are presented in Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0004">4b</a>, and it can be seen that the polarization is generally very low and increases only slightly when increasing the dis-/charge rate from 0.05 C to 2 C, i.e., by a factor of 40. Additionally, we evaluated the long-term cycling stability of these Na|SIPE|NVP cells by subjecting them to 1,000 cycles at 0.2 C (Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0004">4c</a>). The cells displayed a very stable cycling with only minor capacity fading, resulting in a capacity retention of 84.7 % after 1,000 cycles (i.e., 91 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> compared to the initial reversible specific capacity of 107 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). This stable cycling is not least the result of the exceptional average Coulombic efficiency of 99.9 %, which is a remarkably high value for sodium-metal cells. The comparison of selected dis-/charge profiles, depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202308699-fig-0004">4d</a>, reveals that there is no apparent increase in polarization upon cycling. Instead, the voltage plateau gets shorter and the region beyond the plateau gets more sloped, indicating presumably a degradation of the active material - potentially at the outer surface, thus, rendering the de-/sodiation process more sluggish. Nonetheless, the results show that such SIPE is a promising candidate for long-term stable, high-performance SMBs.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202308699-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cc8061ba-76d6-4e42-b825-d0b31f5bdc2a/anie202308699-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cc8061ba-76d6-4e42-b825-d0b31f5bdc2a/anie202308699-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9b563216-ad65-4b62-8a55-0b05d51d5d45/anie202308699-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cc8061ba-76d6-4e42-b825-d0b31f5bdc2a/anie202308699-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202308699-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202308699" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Galvanostatic stripping and plating experiments conducted in symmetric Na||Na cells comprising the EC-doped ionomer as electrolyte at varying current densities at 40 °C. (b) Magnification of selected stripping/plating cycles at the different current densities. (c) Electrochemical impedance spectra recorded for symmetric Na||Na cells upon stripping and plating at a current density of 50 μA cm<sup>−2</sup> at 40 °C; impedance spectra were recorded each 10 stripping/plating cycles, i.e., 1<sup>st</sup> refers to 10 stripping/plating cycles, 3<sup>rd</sup> to 30 stripping/plating cycles etc.; for clarity reasons only each 2<sup>nd</sup> measurement is presented. (d) Determination of t<sub>Na+</sub> via CA with the corresponding impedance spectra recorded before and after the CA experiment as inset along with the equivalent circuit used for the analysis.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202308699-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d10c4843-ef68-49f2-bf11-eaa0cf0218a5/anie202308699-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d10c4843-ef68-49f2-bf11-eaa0cf0218a5/anie202308699-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/de62bd50-c274-406f-bab0-592746a2423b/anie202308699-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d10c4843-ef68-49f2-bf11-eaa0cf0218a5/anie202308699-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202308699-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202308699" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Galvanostatic cycling of Na||NVP cells comprising the EC-doped ionomer as electrolyte at varying C rates (cut-off voltages: 2.5 and 3.9 V; temperature: 40 °C). (b) Selected dis-/charge profiles for the different C rates. (c) Constant current cycling of Na||NVP cells at 0.2 C (temperature: 40 °C). (d) Selected dis-/charge profiles of the 100<sup>th</sup>, 300<sup>th</sup>, 500<sup>th</sup>, and 1000<sup>th</sup> cycle.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, a new sodium SIPE doped with ethylene carbonate has been synthesized and studied as electrolyte for sodium-metal batteries. The self-standing SIPE membranes are characterized by a high ionic conductivity of 2.6 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at 40 °C, a cationic transference number of essentially unity, and an electrochemical stability window of &gt;4 V. Benefitting from a very stable interface with sodium metal and an average Coulombic efficiency of 99.9 %, Na|SIPE|NVP cells showed a capacity retention of about 85 % after 1,000 cycles at 0.2 C and excellent rate capability with still 100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C. The results show that polymer electrolytes are not only a suitable alternative to liquid electrolytes for lithium-metal batteries, but also for sodium-metal batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202308699-sec-0002">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202308699-sec-0002-title"> Supporting Information</h3>
            
            <p>Experimental details and synthesis procedures, including <sup>1</sup>H and <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra of the ionomer and its intermediates; TGA data recorded for pure EC as reference; determination of the limiting current density for the EC-doped ionomer membranes at 40 °C.</p>
            </section>

               <ul class="rlist separator">
